				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Process of achieving spiritual perfection</div>
<p><b>Christian perfection</b> is the name given to a process of achieving spiritual maturity or perfection; theological concepts within many sects of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. The ultimate goal of this process is <a href="/wiki/Divinization_(Christian)" title="Divinization (Christian)">union with God</a> characterized by pure <a href="/wiki/Love_of_God_(Christianity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Love of God (Christianity)">love of God</a> and other people as well as personal holiness or <a href="/wiki/Sanctification" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanctification">sanctification</a>. Other terms used are <b>entire sanctification</b>, <b>perfect love</b>, the <b><a href="/wiki/Baptism_with_the_Holy_Spirit" title="Baptism with the Holy Spirit">baptism with the Holy Spirit</a></b>, the <b>indwelling of the Holy Spirit</b>, <b><a href="/wiki/Baptism_by_fire" title="Baptism by fire">baptism by fire</a></b>, the <b>second blessing</b>, and the <b><a href="/wiki/Second_work_of_grace" title="Second work of grace">second work of grace</a></b>.
</p><p>Understandings of the doctrine of Christian Perfection vary widely between Christian traditions, though these denominational interpretations find basis in Jesus' words recorded in <a href="/wiki/Matthew_5:48" title="Matthew 5:48">Matthew 5:48</a>, "Be ye therefore perfect, even as your Father which is in heaven is perfect" (<a href="/wiki/King_James_Version" title="King James Version">King James Version</a>) but not <a href="/wiki/Matthew_19:21" class="mw-redirect" title="Matthew 19:21">Matthew 19:21</a>, "Jesus said unto him, If thou wilt be perfect, go and sell that thou hast, and give to the poor, and thou shalt have treasure in heaven: and come and follow me."
</p><p>Some protestant denominations, such as the <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Reformed churches</a>, reject the possibility of Christian perfection in this life as contrary to the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Salvation_in_Christianity" title="Salvation in Christianity">salvation</a> by <a href="/wiki/Faith_alone" class="mw-redirect" title="Faith alone">faith alone</a>, holding that deliverance from sin occurs in <a href="/wiki/Glorification#Reformed_Churches" title="Glorification">glorification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hanko_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hanko-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Contrasting to all, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Science" title="Christian Science">Christian Science</a> teaches that as man is made in God's image and likeness (<a href="/wiki/Genesis_1:27" class="mw-redirect" title="Genesis 1:27">Genesis 1:27</a>), "The great spiritual fact must be brought out that man is, not shall be, perfect and immortal"<sup id="cite_ref-Eddy_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eddy-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Catholicism_and_Orthodoxy">In Catholicism and Orthodoxy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: In Catholicism and Orthodoxy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> teaches that Christian perfection is to be sought after by all of the just (righteous).<sup id="cite_ref-Spirago_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spirago-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodoxy</a> situates Christian perfection as a goal for all Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-Jannakos2009_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jannakos2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Protestantism">In Protestantism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: In Protestantism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Perfection is a prominent doctrine within the <a href="/wiki/Methodist" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist">Methodist</a> tradition, in which it is referred to as <i>Christian perfection</i> or <i>entire sanctification</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ogden2008_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogden2008-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Traditional <a href="/wiki/Quakerism" class="mw-redirect" title="Quakerism">Quakerism</a> uses the term <i>perfectionism</i> and teaches that it is the calling of a believer.<sup id="cite_ref-OYM2022_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OYM2022-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fox1661_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fox1661-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The terms "perfect" and "perfection" are drawn from the Greek <i>teleios</i> and <i>teleiōsis</i>, respectively. The root word, <i>telos</i>, means an "end" or "goal". In recent translations, <i>teleios</i> and <i>teleiōsis</i> are often rendered as "mature" and "maturity", respectively, so as not to imply an absolute perfection of no defects. But the words "mature" and "maturity" do not capture the full meaning of "end" or "goal". (Even these recent translations use the word "perfect" when not referring to people, as in James 1:17.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>) In the Christian tradition, <i>teleiōsis</i> has also referred to personal <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/wholeness" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:wholeness">wholeness</a> or health, an unswerving commitment to the goal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201322–23_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201322–23-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_Fathers_and_Medieval_Theologians">Church Fathers and Medieval Theologians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Church Fathers and Medieval Theologians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Divinization_(Christian)" title="Divinization (Christian)">Divinization (Christian)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theosis_(Eastern_Orthodox_theology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Theosis (Eastern Orthodox theology)">Theosis (Eastern Orthodox theology)</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Christ_Taking_Leave_of_the_Apostles.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Christ_Taking_Leave_of_the_Apostles.jpg/200px-Christ_Taking_Leave_of_the_Apostles.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="172" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Christ_Taking_Leave_of_the_Apostles.jpg/300px-Christ_Taking_Leave_of_the_Apostles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Christ_Taking_Leave_of_the_Apostles.jpg/400px-Christ_Taking_Leave_of_the_Apostles.jpg 2x" data-file-width="849" data-file-height="732" /></a><figcaption>In the <a href="/wiki/Farewell_Discourse" title="Farewell Discourse">Farewell Discourse</a> Jesus promised to send the Holy Spirit to his disciples after his departure, depiction from the <i><a href="/wiki/Maest%C3%A0_(Duccio)" title="Maestà (Duccio)">Maesta</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Duccio" title="Duccio">Duccio</a>, 1308–1311.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The roots of the doctrine of Christian perfection lie in the writings of some early <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> theologians considered <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a>: <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Clement_of_Alexandria" title="Clement of Alexandria">Clement of Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Macarius_of_Egypt" title="Macarius of Egypt">Macarius of Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nyssa" title="Gregory of Nyssa">Gregory of Nyssa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Burnett2006_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burnett2006-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Irenaeus wrote about the spiritual transformation that occurred in the believer as the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_in_Christianity" title="Holy Spirit in Christianity">Holy Spirit</a> is to "fit us for God."<sup id="cite_ref-Kaufman2018_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaufman2018-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In antiquity, <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> was commonly referred to as the perfecting of the Christian. This view was expressed by Clement of Alexandria in his work <i><a href="/wiki/Paedagogus" title="Paedagogus">Paedagogus</a></i>: "Being baptized, we are illuminated; illuminated we become children [lit. ‘sons’]; being made children, we are made perfect; being made perfect, we are immortal."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201347_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201347-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In another work, the <i><a href="/wiki/Stromata" title="Stromata">Stromata</a></i>, Clement discussed three stages in Christian life that led to a more mature perfection. The first stage was marked by the change from <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">heathenism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Faith_in_Christianity" title="Faith in Christianity">faith</a> and initiation into the Christian religion. The second stage was marked by a deeper knowledge of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">God</a> that resulted in continuing <a href="/wiki/Repentance_(Christianity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Repentance (Christianity)">repentance</a> from <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_sin" title="Christian views on sin">sin</a> and mastery over <a href="/wiki/Passions_(philosophy)" title="Passions (philosophy)">the passions</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Apatheia" title="Apatheia">apatheia</a></i>). The third stage led to <a href="/wiki/Christian_contemplation" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian contemplation">contemplation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agape" title="Agape">agape</a> love.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201349_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201349-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Origen also proposed his own stages of spiritual ascent beginning with <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Christianity" title="Conversion to Christianity">conversion</a> and ending with perfect union with God in love.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201350_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201350-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gregory of Nyssa defined human perfection as "constant growth in the good". For Gregory, this was brought about by the work of the Holy Spirit and the self-discipline of the Christian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201352_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201352-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Macarius of Egypt taught that all sin could be washed away and that a person could be made perfect in the "span of an hour" while stressing the fact that entire sanctification had a two-fold nature, as "an act and a process".<sup id="cite_ref-Kaufman2018_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaufman2018-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Macarius" title="Pseudo-Macarius">Pseudo-Macarius</a> taught that inner sin was rooted out of the pure in heart, but he also warned against the hidden potential for sin in everyone so that no one should ever say, "Because I am in grace, I am thoroughly freed from sin."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201354–55_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201354–55-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 4th century, the pursuit of the life of perfection was identified with <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">asceticism</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Christian_monasticism" title="Christian monasticism">monasticism</a> and withdrawal from the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978136_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978136-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In the 12th century, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_of_Clairvaux" title="Bernard of Clairvaux">Bernard of Clairvaux</a> developed the idea of the ladder of love in his treatise, <i>On the Love of God</i>. This ladder had four rungs or degrees. The first and lowest degree was love of self for self. The second degree was <a href="/wiki/Love_of_God_in_Christianity" title="Love of God in Christianity">love of God</a> for what he gives. The third degree was love of God for his own sake; it would not be difficult, according to Bernard, for those who truly loved God to keep his <a href="/wiki/Great_Commandment" title="Great Commandment">commandments</a>. The fourth degree was love of self only for God's sake; it was believed that this degree of perfection in love was only rarely achieved before death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201363–64_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201363–64-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> wrote of three possible levels of perfection.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The first, absolute perfection, is where God is loved as much as he can be loved; only God himself can be this perfect. The second level, where love for God fills a person constantly, is possible after death but not in life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201365_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201365-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The lowest level of perfection was thought to be possible to achieve while living. Theologian <a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Noble_(theologian)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Thomas Noble (theologian) (page does not exist)">Thomas Noble</a> described Aquinas' view of this level of perfection as follows:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>All Christians have the grace of <i><a href="/wiki/Charity_(virtue)" class="mw-redirect" title="Charity (virtue)">caritas</a></i> infused into them at baptism and this love for God excludes all <a href="/wiki/Mortal_sin" title="Mortal sin">mortal sins</a>. Such sins are not impossible, and, if committed, require the grace of <a href="/wiki/Penance" title="Penance">penance</a>, but Christians do not live committing flagrant acts of intentional sin contrary to their love for God. That is incompatible with the state of <a href="/wiki/Grace_in_Christianity" title="Grace in Christianity">grace</a>. But those who are no longer beginners, but making progress in the life of perfection, come to the point where everything contrary to being wholly in love with God is excluded: they love God with all their hearts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201366_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201366-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>According to the standard formulation of the process of Christian perfection, as formulated by <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_the_Pseudo-Areopagite" class="mw-redirect" title="Dionysius the Pseudo-Areopagite">Dionysius the Pseudo-Areopagite</a> (late 5th to early 6th century),<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CP_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CP-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> there are <a href="/wiki/State_(theology)" class="mw-redirect" title="State (theology)">three stages</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CE_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CE-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CP_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CP-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Catharsis" title="Catharsis">Katharsis</a></i> or purification;</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Theoria" class="mw-redirect" title="Theoria">Theoria</a></i> or illumination, also called "natural" or "acquired contemplation;"</li>
<li><i>Union</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Theosis_(Eastern_Christian_theology)" title="Theosis (Eastern Christian theology)">Theosis</a></i>; also called "infused" or "higher contemplation"; indwelling in God; vision of God; deification; <a href="/wiki/Henosis" title="Henosis">union with God</a></li></ul>
<p>Daniel L. Burnett, a professor at <a href="/wiki/Wesley_Biblical_Seminary" title="Wesley Biblical Seminary">Wesley Biblical Seminary</a>, writes that:<sup id="cite_ref-2006Burnett_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2006Burnett-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Views compatible with the Wesleyan understanding of entire sanctification were carried forward in later times by men like the medieval Catholic priest <a href="/wiki/Thomas_a_Kempis" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas a Kempis">Thomas a Kempis</a>, the Protestant Reformers Caspel Schwenkfeld and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Munzer" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Munzer">Thomas Munzer</a>, the Dutch theologian <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Arminius" title="Jacobus Arminius">Jacobus Arminius</a>, the German Pietist Phillip Jacob Spener, the Quaker founder <a href="/wiki/George_Fox" title="George Fox">George Fox</a>, the Anglican bishop <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Taylor" title="Jeremy Taylor">Jeremy Taylor</a>, and the English devotional writer <a href="/wiki/William_Law" title="William Law">William Law</a>. Many of these influences fed into [John] Wesley's heritage and laid the foundation for the development of his thought. In fact, the concept of entire sanctification is so pervasive throughout church history that it can accurately be said that virtually all the major traditions—Orthodox, Catholic, Reformed, and Anglican—played some part in shaping Wesley's passion for holiness.<sup id="cite_ref-2006Burnett_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2006Burnett-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Catholic_teaching">Roman Catholic teaching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Roman Catholic teaching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For additional information, see <a href="/wiki/Catholic_spirituality" title="Catholic spirituality">Catholic spirituality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universal_call_to_holiness" title="Universal call to holiness">Universal call to holiness</a>.</div>
<p>According to the teaching of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>, something is perfect when nothing is lacking in its nature or purpose. The ultimate purpose of Man is union with God, also called <a href="/wiki/Divinization_(Christian)" title="Divinization (Christian)">divinization</a>. This is accomplished on earth by grace and in <a href="/wiki/Heaven_in_Christianity" title="Heaven in Christianity">heaven</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Beatific_vision" title="Beatific vision">beatific vision</a>. Perfect union with God while on earth is impossible; therefore, absolute perfection is reserved for heaven.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDevine1911_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDevine1911-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Roman Catholic Church teaches that Christian perfection is a spiritual union with God that is attainable in this life. It is not absolute perfection as it exists alongside human misery, rebellious passions, and <a href="/wiki/Venial_sin" title="Venial sin">venial sin</a>. Christian perfection consists of charity or love, since it is this virtue that unites the <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a> to God. It is not just the possession and preservation of <a href="/wiki/Sanctifying_grace" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanctifying grace">sanctifying grace</a>, since perfection is determined by one's action—the actual practicing of charity or the service of God.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDevine1911_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDevine1911-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The more charity a person possesses, the greater the perfection of the soul. A person who is perfect in so far as being free from <a href="/wiki/Mortal_sin" title="Mortal sin">mortal sin</a> obtains salvation and can be called just, holy, and perfect. A person who is perfect insofar as also being free from venial sin and all affections which separate a person from God is in a state of active service and love of God. This is the perfect <a href="/wiki/Great_Commandment" title="Great Commandment">fulfillment of the law</a>—loving God and loving other people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDevine1911_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDevine1911-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Roman Catholic Church teaches that Christian perfection is something all should pursue in light of Jesus' injunction in Matthew 5:48.<sup id="cite_ref-Spirago_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spirago-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> There is also, however, what is called "religious perfection", which is pursued by those committed to living <a href="/wiki/Consecrated_life" title="Consecrated life">religious life</a>, such as members of <a href="/wiki/Catholic_religious_orders" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic religious orders">religious orders</a>. All Roman Catholics are obliged to attain perfection by observing the commandments, but religious life imposes a more exacting obligation, requiring the religious to also observe the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_counsels" title="Evangelical counsels">evangelical counsels</a> (also known as "counsels of perfection") of <a href="/wiki/Voluntary_poverty" class="mw-redirect" title="Voluntary poverty">poverty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vow_of_chastity" class="mw-redirect" title="Vow of chastity">chastity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vow_of_obedience" title="Vow of obedience">obedience</a>. The evangelical counsels are believed to promote perfection in two ways. They remove the obstacles to perfection—<a href="/wiki/Lust" title="Lust">lust</a> of the eyes, lust of the <a href="/wiki/Flesh_(theology)" title="Flesh (theology)">flesh</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Pride" title="Pride">pride</a> of life. They also increase a person's love of God by freeing the affections from earthly ties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDevine1911_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDevine1911-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Way_of_Perfection" class="mw-redirect" title="Way of Perfection">El Camino de Perfección</a></i> is a method for making progress in the contemplative life written by Saint <a href="/wiki/Teresa_of_%C3%81vila" title="Teresa of Ávila">Teresa of Ávila</a> for the sisters of her reformed convent of the <a href="/wiki/Discalced_Carmelites" title="Discalced Carmelites">Discalced Carmelites</a>. St. Teresa was a major figure of the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> in 16th century Spain. <i>Christian Perfection</i> is also the title of a book written by theologian <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9ginald_Garrigou-Lagrange" title="Réginald Garrigou-Lagrange">Réginald Garrigou-Lagrange</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Perfectae_Caritatis" title="Perfectae Caritatis">Perfectae Caritatis</a></i>, the Decree on the Adaptation and Renewal of Religious Life, is one of the shorter documents issued by the <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">Second Vatican Council</a>. Approved by vote of 2,321 to 4 of the bishops assembled at the council, the decree was promulgated by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_VI" title="Pope Paul VI">Pope Paul VI</a> on October 28, 1965. As is customary for Church documents, the title is taken from the Latin <a href="/wiki/Incipit" title="Incipit">incipit</a> of the decree: "Of Perfect Charity".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Orthodox_teaching">Eastern Orthodox teaching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Eastern Orthodox teaching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Church</a> teaches that "perfection is possible for us as human beings as long as we understand it in its proper, dynamic sense" and that humans are "made for Theosis, for the deification ('divinizing') of the totality of our being, body, mind, heart, and soul".<sup id="cite_ref-Jannakos2009_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jannakos2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> This is in accordance with the writings of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Paul">Saint Paul</a> that encourage Christians to seek after the righteousness of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus</a> to be transformed from "one degree of glory to another".<sup id="cite_ref-Jannakos2009_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jannakos2009-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Byzantine Orthodox hagiographer and hymnodist St <a href="/wiki/Symeon_the_Metaphrast" title="Symeon the Metaphrast">Symeon the Metaphrast</a> (10th c.) declared:<sup id="cite_ref-Hayward_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayward-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Those who deny the possibility of perfection inflict the greatest damage on the soul in three ways. First, they manifestly disbelieve the inspired Scriptures. Then, because they do not make the greatest and fullest goal of Christianity their own, and so do not aspire to attain it, they can have no longing and diligence, no hunger and thirst for righteousness (cf <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Matthew#5:6" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Matthew">Matt. 5:6</a>); on the contrary, content with outward show and behavior and with minor accomplishments of this kind, they abandon that blessed expectation together with the pursuit of perfection and of the total purification of the passions. Third, thinking they have reached the goal when they have acquired a few virtues, and not pressing on to the true goal, not only are they incapable of having any humility, poverty and contrition of heart but, justifying themselves on the grounds that they have already arrived, they make no efforts to progress and grow day by day. People who think it is impossible to attain through the Spirit the 'new creation' of the pure heart (cf <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/2_Corinthians#5:17" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/2 Corinthians">2 Cor. 5:17</a>) are rightly and explicitly likened by the apostle to those who, because of their unbelief, were found unworthy of entering the promised land and whose bodies on that account 'were left lying in the desert' (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Hebrews#3:17" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Hebrews">Heb. 3:17</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Hayward_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayward-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anabaptist_teaching">Anabaptist teaching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Anabaptist teaching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Anabaptist_Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabaptist Christian">Anabaptist Christians</a> (inclusive of the <a href="/wiki/Mennonite" class="mw-redirect" title="Mennonite">Mennonite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amish" title="Amish">Amish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hutterite" class="mw-redirect" title="Hutterite">Hutterite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bruderhof" class="mw-redirect" title="Bruderhof">Bruderhof</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schwarzenau_Brethren" title="Schwarzenau Brethren">Schwarzenau Brethren</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Brethren" title="River Brethren">River Brethren</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Christian_Church" title="Apostolic Christian Church">Apostolic Christian</a> denominations) believe that "because they have voluntarily chosen to follow Christ as their only authority", they can be successful in their pursuit of Christian perfection.<sup id="cite_ref-Chernus_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernus-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Professor of Religious Studies <a href="/wiki/Ira_Chernus" title="Ira Chernus">Ira Chernus</a> explicates Anabaptist doctrine:<sup id="cite_ref-Chernus_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernus-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>… Anabaptists put special stress on the power of faith to produce good works and a more moral life. … they are guided by the concluding injunction of the Sermon on the Mount: "You shall be perfect, as your Heavenly Father is perfect." They strive for perfection; they view their church as the visible body of Christ, which must be, and can be, a "spotless congregation." As one of their greatest early leaders, Menno Simmons, said: "The reborn willfully here sin no more."<sup id="cite_ref-Chernus_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chernus-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In particular, the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Christian_Church" title="Apostolic Christian Church">Apostolic Christian Church</a> is "distinguished by its emphasis on entire sanctification".<sup id="cite_ref-Jones1974_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones1974-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wesleyan_teaching">Wesleyan teaching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Wesleyan teaching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background-color:#f8e0e0;;background:#f8e0e0;">Organization</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Connexionalism" title="Connexionalism">Connexionalism</a></li>

<span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/16px-P_christianity.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="14" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/24px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/32px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="360" /></a></span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity&#32;portal</a>

href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output <p><span class="anchor" id="Wesleyan_theology"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="John_Wesley">John Wesley</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: John Wesley">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In traditional <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> and <a href="/wiki/High_church" title="High church">high church</a> <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>, perfection was viewed as a gift bestowed on righteous persons only after their death (see <a href="/wiki/Glorification#Reformed_Churches" title="Glorification">Glorification</a>). <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">John Wesley</a>, the founder of <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodism</a>, was responsible for reviving the idea of spiritual perfection in <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978454–55_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978454–55-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Wesley's views were elaborated in <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/A_Plain_Account_of_Christian_Perfection" class="extiw" title="wikisource:A Plain Account of Christian Perfection"><i>A Plain Account of Christian Perfection</i></a>, published in 1777.
</p><p>According to Noble, Wesley transformed Christian perfection as found in church tradition by interpreting it through a Protestant lens that understood <a href="/wiki/Sanctification" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanctification">sanctification</a> in light of <a href="/wiki/Justification_(theology)" title="Justification (theology)">justification</a> by grace <a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">through faith</a> working by love.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201373_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201373-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Wesley believed that <a href="/wiki/Regeneration_(theology)" title="Regeneration (theology)">regeneration</a> (or the <a href="/wiki/Born_again" title="Born again">new birth</a>), which occurred simultaneously with justification, was the beginning of sanctification.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201380_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201380-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> From his reading of <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Romans" title="Epistle to the Romans">Romans</a> 6 and <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_John" title="First Epistle of John">1 John</a> 3:9, Wesley concluded that a consequence of the new birth was power over sin. In a sermon titled "Christian Perfection", Wesley preached that "A Christian is so far perfect as not to commit sin."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201381,_84_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201381,_84-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
"The term “sinless perfection” was one which Wesley never used because of its ambiguity."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_William_Fletcher" title="John William Fletcher">John William Fletcher</a>, an early Methodist divine who John Wesley chose to lead the Methodist movement if he died, clarified the Wesleyan doctrine by stating "that the doctrine of an evangelically sinless perfection is truly Scriptural."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tyerman2017_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tyerman2017-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> And "I say evangelically sinless, because, without the word evangelically, the phrase “sinless perfection” gives an occasion of cavilling to those who seek it."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Methodists are able to hold this doctrine based upon Wesley's definition of actual sin:</p><blockquote><p>"Nothing is sin, strictly speaking, but a voluntary transgression of a known law of God. Therefore, every voluntary breach of the law of love is sin; and nothing else, if we speak properly. To strain the matter farther is only to make way for Calvinism. There may be ten thousand wandering thoughts, and forgetful intervals, without any breach of love, though not without transgressing the Adamic law. But Calvinists would fain confound these together. Let love fill your heart, and it is enough!"<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Involuntary transgressions (such as those arising from ignorance, error, and evil tempers), according to Wesley, were not properly called sins.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978455_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978455-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, regenerated Christians would continue to be guilty of involuntary transgressions and would need to practice regular <a href="/wiki/Confession_(religion)" title="Confession (religion)">confession</a>. Furthermore, Christians continued to face <a href="/wiki/Temptation" title="Temptation">temptation</a>, and Wesley acknowledged that it was possible for a regenerated Christian to commit voluntary sin (if, in the words of Noble, the Christian ceased "actively trusting in God through Christ and living in the divine presence"), which would also necessitate confession of sin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201382_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201382-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The power over sin received at regeneration was just the lowest stage of Christian perfection according to Wesley. Based on 1 John 2, Wesley proposed three stages in the Christian life: little children, young men, and finally fathers.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Young men were defined as those who had experienced victory over temptation and evil thoughts. Fathers were defined as mature Christians who were filled with the love of God.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201384_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201384-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
Wesley believed this last stage of Christian maturity was made possible by what he called entire sanctification (a phrase derived from <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_to_the_Thessalonians" title="First Epistle to the Thessalonians">First Thessalonians</a> 5:23). In Wesley's theology, entire sanctification was a <a href="/wiki/Second_work_of_grace" title="Second work of grace">second work of grace</a> received by faith that removed inbred or <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">original sin</a>, and this allowed the Christian to enter a state of perfect love—"Love excluding sin" as stated in the sermon "The Scripture Way of Salvation".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201386_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201386-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stokes1971_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stokes1971-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Wesley described it as having "purity of intention", "dedicating all the life to God", "loving God with all our heart", and as being the "renewal of the heart in the whole <a href="/wiki/Image_of_God" title="Image of God">image of God</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201386_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201386-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> A life of perfect love meant living in a way that was centered on loving God and one's neighbor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201387_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201387-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In his Sermon called "The Circumcision of the Heart" Wesley described it like this:</p><blockquote><p>"It is that habitual disposition of soul which, in the sacred writings, is termed holiness; and which directly implies, the being cleansed from sin, 'from all filthiness both of flesh and spirit;' and, by consequence, the being endued with those virtues which were also in Christ Jesus; the being so 'renewed in the spirit of our mind,' as to be 'perfect as our Father in heaven is perfect.'"<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Even this was not an absolute perfection. The entirely sanctified Christian was perfect in love, meaning that the heart is undivided in its love for God or that it loves nothing that conflicts with its love for God. Christians perfected in love were still subject to conditions of <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_man" title="Fall of man">the Fall</a> and liable to commit unintentional transgressions. In consequence, these Christians still had to depend on <a href="/wiki/Forgiveness" title="Forgiveness">forgiveness</a> through Christ's <a href="/wiki/Atonement_in_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Atonement in Christianity">atonement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoble201390–91_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoble201390–91-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> However, with Wesley's concept of sin, he did believe in freedom from sin. In fact, he described it like this: "Certainly sanctification (in the proper sense) is “an instantaneous deliverance from all sin;” and includes “an instantaneous power then given.”<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Wesley's concept of Christian perfection had both gradual and instantaneous elements. In his 1765 sermon "The Scripture Way of Salvation", Wesley emphasized the instantaneous side, stating, "Do you believe we are sanctified by faith? Be true, then, to your principle and look for this blessing just as you are, neither better nor worse; as a poor sinner that has still nothing to pay, nothing to plead but 'Christ died'. And if you look for it as you are, then expect it now".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978456_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978456-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In "Thoughts on Christian Perfection" (1759), Wesley stressed the gradual aspect of perfection, writing that it was to be received "in a zealous keeping of all the commandments; in watchfulness and painfulness; in denying ourselves and taking up our cross daily; as well as in earnest prayer and fasting and a close attendance on all the ordinances of God . . . it is true we receive it by simple faith; but God does not, will not, give that faith unless we seek it with all diligence in the way which he hath ordained".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978455_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978455-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In addition, Wesley also believed that Christian perfection, once received, might be forfeited.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978456_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe-Jones1978456-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>John Wesley taught <a href="/wiki/Outward_holiness" title="Outward holiness">outward holiness</a> as an expression of "inward transformation" and theologians in the Wesleyan Methodist tradition have noted that the observance of standards of dress and behaviour should follow the <a href="/wiki/Born_again#Methodism" title="Born again">New Birth</a> as an act of obedience to God.<sup id="cite_ref-Thornton2008_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thornton2008-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mainline_Methodism">Mainline Methodism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mainline Methodism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mourners%27_Bench_at_Mt._Zion_United_Methodist_Church.jpeg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Mourners%27_Bench_at_Mt._Zion_United_Methodist_Church.jpeg/350px-Mourners%27_Bench_at_Mt._Zion_United_Methodist_Church.jpeg" decoding="async" width="350" height="233" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Mourners%27_Bench_at_Mt._Zion_United_Methodist_Church.jpeg/525px-Mourners%27_Bench_at_Mt._Zion_United_Methodist_Church.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Mourners%27_Bench_at_Mt._Zion_United_Methodist_Church.jpeg/700px-Mourners%27_Bench_at_Mt._Zion_United_Methodist_Church.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="4256" data-file-height="2832" /></a><figcaption>Methodists often seek the <a href="/wiki/Born_again" title="Born again">new birth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Entire_sanctification" class="mw-redirect" title="Entire sanctification">entire sanctification</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Mourners%27_bench" class="mw-redirect" title="Mourners&#39; bench">mourners' bench</a> or <a href="/wiki/Altar_rails" class="mw-redirect" title="Altar rails">chancel rails</a> during services held in local churches, <a href="/wiki/Tent_revival" title="Tent revival">tent revivals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camp_meeting" title="Camp meeting">camp meetings</a> (pictured are people praying at Mount Zion United Methodist Church in <a href="/wiki/Pasadena,_Maryland" title="Pasadena, Maryland">Pasadena</a>).</figcaption></figure>
<p>Calling it "the grand depositum" of the Methodist faith, Wesley specifically taught that the propagation of the doctrine of entire sanctification to the rest of <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> was the main reason that God raised up the Methodists in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-DaviesGeorge2017_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DaviesGeorge2017-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> After Wesley's death, his teachings on Christian perfection remained important to the Methodist church, but, according to historian <a href="/wiki/David_Bebbington" class="mw-redirect" title="David Bebbington">David Bebbington</a>, "the tradition fell into decay."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBebbington1989153_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBebbington1989153-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> As later generations of Methodists sought greater respectability in the eyes of other Christian denominations, some turned to "a watered-down version" of the doctrine outlined by <a href="/wiki/William_Arthur_(minister)" title="William Arthur (minister)">William Arthur</a> (who served as Secretary of the <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_Methodist_Church_(Great_Britain)" title="Wesleyan Methodist Church (Great Britain)">Wesleyan Methodist Missionary Society</a>) in his popular work <i>The Tongue of Fire</i>, published in 1856. While Arthur encouraged readers to pray for a greater experience of the Holy Spirit, he de-emphasized the instantaneous aspect of Christian perfection. According to Bebbington, this eliminated the distinctiveness of Wesleyan entire sanctification, and by the 1860s, the idea that Christian perfection was a decisive second blessing or stage in Christian sanctification had fallen out of favor among some Methodists, though not all Methodists, as academic institutions affiliated with mainline Methodism such as <a href="/wiki/Asbury_Theological_Seminary" title="Asbury Theological Seminary">Asbury Theological Seminary</a>, Methodist <a href="/wiki/Camp_meeting" title="Camp meeting">camp meetings</a>, and other Holiness Methodist associations within the Church continued to be a beacon for the <a href="/wiki/Holiness_movement" title="Holiness movement">holiness movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBebbington1989153_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBebbington1989153-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Winn_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Winn-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In contemporary Methodist Churches, Christian perfection remains official doctrine and both its gradual and instantaneous aspects are recognized.<sup id="cite_ref-Catechism2013_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catechism2013-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <i>A Catechism for the use of the people called Methodists</i> teaches:<sup id="cite_ref-Catechism2013_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catechism2013-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Through the Holy Spirit God has given us His love so that we may love Him in return with all our heart, soul, mind and strength, and our neighbour as ourselves. This is a gift offered to all Christians, and by responding we affirm that there is no limit to what the grace of God is able to do in a human life. By giving us the Holy Spirit, God assures us of His love for us and enables us to love as He, in Christ, loves us. When God's love is perfected in us, we so represent Christ to our neighbours that they see Him in us without hindrance from us. Perfect love, as Christian perfect is often called, is the result of, and can only be maintained by, complete dependence on Jesus Christ. It is given either gradually or at one moment...<sup id="cite_ref-Catechism2013_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catechism2013-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Candidates for <a href="/wiki/Ordination" title="Ordination">ordination</a> are asked the following question, "Do you expect to be made perfect in love in this life?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell199962_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell199962-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Church_of_Great_Britain" title="Methodist Church of Great Britain">Methodist Church of Great Britain</a>, the distinctive Wesleyan teachings are summed up in the phrase "All need to be saved; all can be saved; all can know they are saved; all can be saved to the uttermost" (the word "uttermost" referring to Christian perfection).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWainwright1999374_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWainwright1999374-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Confession_of_Faith_(United_Methodist)" title="Confession of Faith (United Methodist)">Confession of Faith</a>, one of the Doctrinal Standards of <a href="/wiki/The_United_Methodist_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="The United Methodist Church">The United Methodist Church</a>, teaches that entire sanctification may be bestowed upon the believer gradually or instantaneously:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We believe sanctification is the work of God's grace through the Word and the Spirit, by which those who have been born again are cleansed from sin in their thoughts, words and acts, and are enabled to live in accordance with God's will, and to strive for holiness without which no one will see the Lord.
</p><p>Entire sanctification is a state of perfect love, righteousness and true holiness which every regenerate believer may obtain by being delivered from the power of sin, by loving God with all the heart, soul, mind and strength, and by loving one's neighbor as one's self. Through faith in Jesus Christ this gracious gift may be received in this life both gradually and instantaneously, and should be sought earnestly by every child of God.
</p><p>
We believe this experience does not deliver us from the infirmities, ignorance, and mistakes common to man, nor from the possibilities of further sin. The Christian must continue on guard against spiritual pride and seek to gain victory over every temptation to sin. He must respond wholly to the will of God so that sin will lose its power over him; and the world, the flesh, and the devil are put under his feet. Thus he rules over these enemies with watchfulness through the power of the Holy Spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-Ogden2008_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogden2008-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>James Heidinger II, former president of the <a href="/wiki/Confessing_Movement#Methodist" title="Confessing Movement">Good News movement</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">evangelical</a> caucus within the United Methodist Church, has emphasized the significance of the doctrine of entire sanctification within Methodism: "There is no question about the importance of the doctrine of perfection in the history of Methodism. Wesley believed that this emphasis was a peculiar heritage given to the Methodists in trust for the whole Church."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeidinger2013_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeidinger2013-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> However, he has also noted that uncertainty, among some, exists within the denomination over the teaching: "Our discomfort with this doctrine today is seen in services of ordination when candidates are asked, 'Are you going on to perfection?' Our misunderstanding about this often brings uneasy chuckles and quick disclaimers that we certainly don’t claim to be 'perfect' in our Christian life."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeidinger2013_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeidinger2013-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Brian Beck, former <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_Methodist_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of the Methodist Conference">President of the Methodist Conference in Britain</a>, expressed his personal opinion in 2000 that "The doctrine [of sanctification] remains with us in Charles Wesley's hymns, but the formative framework, and even, I suspect, the spiritual intention, have largely gone".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacquiban200619_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacquiban200619-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Writing on the need for improved spiritual formation within the British Methodist Church and the US-based United Methodist Church, Methodist theologian <a href="/wiki/Randy_L._Maddox" title="Randy L. Maddox">Randy L. Maddox</a> commented that the terms "holiness of heart and life" and "Christian Perfection" were considered "prone to moralistic, static and unrealistic connotations, resulting in the growing uncomfortableness with and neglect of this aspect of our Wesleyan heritage".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaddox2006183_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaddox2006183-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The Rev. Dr. Kevin M. Watson, a United Methodist clergyman and Assistant Professor of Historical Theology and Wesleyan Studies at Seattle Pacific University, implores fellow pastors: "Teaching and preaching the possibility of being made perfect in love for God and neighbor, and seeking to actually become entirely sanctified are the reasons Methodism was 'raised up.' May we remember who we are and why the Holy Spirit brought us to life."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Global_Methodist_Church" title="Global Methodist Church">Global Methodist Church</a> enshrines the doctrine of entire sanctification in its official catechism, teaching that "Entire sanctification is a state of perfect love, righteousness, and true holiness which every regenerate believer may obtain."<sup id="cite_ref-GMC2022_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GMC2022-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> It teaches that Christian perfection may be "received in this life either gradually or instantaneously" and that it should be "sought earnestly by every child of God." In order to maintain this  state of holiness, the believer must "respond wholly to the will of God so that sin will lose its power over us; and the world, the flesh, and the devil are put under our feet."<sup id="cite_ref-GMC2022_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GMC2022-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For John Wesley, the preaching of Christian perfection was crucial to the spiritual health of a Methodist church: he taught that "Where Christian perfection is not strongly and explicitly preached, there is seldom any remarkable blessing from God; and consequently little addition to the society, and little life in the members of it."<sup id="cite_ref-Brown2011_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2011-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> As such, he urged ministers: "Till you press the believers to expect full salvation [entire sanctification] now, you must not look for any revival."<sup id="cite_ref-Brown2011_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2011-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holiness_movement">Holiness movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Holiness movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Holiness_movement" title="Holiness movement">Holiness movement</a></div>
<p>In the 19th century, there were Methodists who sought to revitalize the doctrine of Christian perfection or holiness, which had, in the words of religion scholar <a href="/wiki/Randall_Balmer" title="Randall Balmer">Randall Balmer</a>, "faded into the background" as mainline Methodists gained respectability and became solidly middle class. While it originated as a revival movement within the <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Episcopal_Church" title="Methodist Episcopal Church">Methodist Episcopal Church</a> and many adherents of the holiness movement remained within mainline Methodism, the holiness movement grew to be <a href="/wiki/Interdenominational" class="mw-redirect" title="Interdenominational">interdenominational</a> and gave rise to a number of <a href="/wiki/Wesleyanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Wesleyanism">Wesleyan</a>-holiness denominations, including the <a href="/wiki/Free_Methodist_Church" title="Free Methodist Church">Free Methodist Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Nazarene" title="Church of the Nazarene">Church of the Nazarene</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_God_(Anderson,_Indiana)" title="Church of God (Anderson, Indiana)">Church of God (Anderson, Indiana)</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Salvation_Army" title="The Salvation Army">The Salvation Army</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_Methodist_Church_(United_States)" title="Wesleyan Methodist Church (United States)">Wesleyan Methodist Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Winn_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Winn-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBalmer2002339–40_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBalmer2002339–40-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swatos1998_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swatos1998-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
An early promoter of holiness was American Methodist <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_Palmer" title="Phoebe Palmer">Phoebe Palmer</a>. Through her evangelism and writings, Palmer articulated an "altar theology" that outlined a "shorter way" to entire sanctification, achieved through placing oneself on a metaphorical <a href="/wiki/Altar" title="Altar">altar</a> by <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrificing</a> worldly desires. As long as the Christian placed themselves on the altar and had faith that it was God's will to accomplish sanctification, the Christian could be assured that God would sanctify them. In the words of historian Jeffrey Williams, "Palmer made sanctification an instantaneous act accomplished through the exercise of faith."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams2010150_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams2010150-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Many holiness denominations require pastors to profess that they have already experienced entire sanctification.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell1996237_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell1996237-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> This emphasis on the instantaneous nature of Christian perfection rather than its gradual side is a defining feature of the Wesleyan-holiness movement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossLivingstone2005-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The Discipline of the <a href="/wiki/Bible_Methodist_Connection_of_Churches" title="Bible Methodist Connection of Churches">Bible Methodist Connection of Churches</a> thus teaches that:<sup id="cite_ref-BMCC2018_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BMCC2018-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>"We believe that entire sanctification is that work of the Holy Spirit by which the child of God is cleansed from inherited depravity and empowered for more effective service through faith in Jesus Christ. It is subsequent to regeneration and is accomplished in a moment of time when the believer presents himself a living sacrifice, holy and acceptable unto God. The Spirit-filled believer is thus enabled to love God with an undivided heart."<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>A second defining emphasis of the Holiness movement is the complete destruction and eradication of the sin nature. H. Orton Wiley, the premier Holiness systematic theologian, quotes R. T. Williams explaining:</p><blockquote><p>"It is folly to try to pass as a believer in holiness and at the same time question its doctrine of eradication. There cannot be such a thing as holiness in its final analysis without the eradication of sin. Holiness and suppression are incompatible terms. “The old man” and counteraction make a pale and sickly kind of holiness doctrine. It is holiness and eradication or holiness not at all."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Another key aspect of the Holiness movement is their close adherence to Wesley's definition of sin. Wesley stated in a letter:</p><blockquote><p>"Nothing is sin, strictly speaking, but a voluntary transgression of a known law of God. Therefore, every voluntary breach of the law of love is sin; and nothing else, if we speak properly. To strain the matter farther is only to make way for Calvinism. There may be ten thousand wandering thoughts, and forgetful intervals, without any breach of love, though not without transgressing the Adamic law. But Calvinists would fain confound these together. Let love fill your heart, and it is enough!"<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>With this understanding of sin, clergy aligned with the <a href="/wiki/Holiness_movement" title="Holiness movement">holiness movement</a> teach the possibility of complete freedom from all sin, both inward and outward, as expressed by <a href="/wiki/John_William_Fletcher" title="John William Fletcher">John Fletcher</a>'s statement "He who is possessed of love, is free from all sin."<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holiness_Pentecostalism">Holiness Pentecostalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Holiness Pentecostalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Holiness Pentecostal</b> denominations, also known as Wesleyan Pentecostals or Methodistic Pentecostals are <a href="/wiki/Pentecostalism" title="Pentecostalism">Pentecostals</a> that believe in entire sanctification as a second work of grace.<sup id="cite_ref-Synan2012_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Synan2012-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Inheriting <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_theology" title="Wesleyan theology">Wesleyan–Arminian</a> theology from the <a href="/wiki/Holiness_movement" title="Holiness movement">holiness movement</a> within <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodism</a>, Holiness Pentecostals are the original branch of Pentecostalism,<sup id="cite_ref-Synan2012_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Synan2012-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> and these denominations include the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Faith_Church" title="Apostolic Faith Church">Apostolic Faith Church</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;A&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Congregational_Holiness_Church" title="Congregational Holiness Church">Congregational Holiness Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Free_Gospel_Church" title="Free Gospel Church">Free Gospel Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Pentecostal_Holiness_Church" title="International Pentecostal Holiness Church">International Pentecostal Holiness Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_God_(Cleveland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Church of God (Cleveland)">Church of God (Cleveland)</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_God_in_Christ" title="Church of God in Christ">Church of God in Christ</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_(Original)_Church_of_God" title="The (Original) Church of God">The (Original) Church of God</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anderson2004_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2004-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Free_Gospel_Bible_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Gospel Bible Institute">Free Gospel Bible Institute</a> (FGBI) is a <a href="/wiki/Bible_college" title="Bible college">Bible college</a> that trains many Holiness Pentecostal clergy.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> For Holiness Pentecostals, entire sanctification is the <a href="/wiki/Second_work_of_grace" title="Second work of grace">second work of grace</a> in a series of three distinct blessings that Christians experience. The first work of grace is conversion (the new birth) and the third work of grace is the <a href="/wiki/Baptism_in_the_Holy_Spirit" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptism in the Holy Spirit">baptism in the Holy Spirit</a> (which is marked by <a href="/wiki/Speaking_in_tongues" title="Speaking in tongues">speaking in tongues</a>). Methodism (inclusive of the holiness movement) teaches two works of grace—the New Birth and Entire Sanctification, while Holiness Pentecostals add a third work of grace, Baptism in the Holy Spirit, to this sequence. According to church historian and theologian Ted A. Campbell, this three-part pattern is often explained by stating "the Holy Spirit cannot fill an unclean vessel", so the cleansing of the heart that takes place in entire sanctification is necessary before a person can be filled or baptized with the Holy Spirit. The <a href="/wiki/Testimony" title="Testimony">testimony</a> of those who attended the <a href="/wiki/Azusa_Street_Revival" title="Azusa Street Revival">Azusa Street Revival</a> was "I am saved, sanctified, and filled with the Holy Ghost" in reference to the three works of grace of Holiness Pentecostals, the oldest branch of Pentecostalism.<sup id="cite_ref-Synan2012_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Synan2012-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Finished_Work" title="Finished Work">Finished Work Pentecostal denominations</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Assemblies_of_God" title="Assemblies of God">Assemblies of God</a>, reject the doctrine of entire sanctification.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell1996237_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell1996237-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Levinson1996_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levinson1996-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quaker_teaching">Quaker teaching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_perfection&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Quaker teaching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><table class="sidebar sidebar-collapse nomobile nowraplinks plainlist" style="border-collapse:collapse;"><tbody><tr><td class="sidebar-pretitle">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Quakers" title="Category:Quakers">a series</a> on</td></tr><tr><th class="sidebar-title-with-pretitle" style="background-color:#efefef"><span style="font-size:120%"><a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakerism</a></span></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Fox.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="George Fox"><img alt="George Fox" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/George_Fox.jpg/100px-George_Fox.jpg" decoding="async" width="100" height="117" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/George_Fox.jpg/150px-George_Fox.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/George_Fox.jpg 2x" data-file-width="187" data-file-height="218" /></a></span><div class="sidebar-caption"><a href="/wiki/George_Fox" title="George Fox">George Fox</a>, founder of the Quakers</div></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
